</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Ancient_Rome_and_the_fall_of_the_Republic" title="Template:Ancient Rome and the fall of the Republic"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Ancient_Rome_and_the_fall_of_the_Republic" title="Template talk:Ancient Rome and the fall of the Republic"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Ancient_Rome_and_the_fall_of_the_Republic&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Marcus Tullius Cicero</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[a]</a></sup> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">SISS</span>-ə-roh</i></a>; <small>Latin: </small><span class="IPA" lang="la-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[ˈmaːr.kʊs ˈtʊl.lʲi.jʊs ˈkɪ.kɛ.roː]</a></span>; 3 January 106 BC – 7 December 43 BC) was a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> statesman, lawyer, scholar, philosopher, and <a href="/wiki/Academic_skepticism" title="Academic skepticism">academic skeptic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> who tried to uphold <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optimate" title="Optimate">optimate</a> principles during <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Roman_Republic" title="Crisis of the Roman Republic">the political crises</a> that led to the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFergusonBalsdon_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFergusonBalsdon-5">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Writings_of_Cicero" title="Writings of Cicero">His extensive writings</a> include treatises on <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a>, philosophy and politics, and he is considered one of Rome's greatest <a href="/wiki/Orator" title="Orator">orators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prose" title="Prose">prose</a> stylists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> He came from a wealthy <a href="/wiki/Municipium" title="Municipium">municipal</a> family of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_equestrian_order" title="Roman equestrian order">Roman equestrian order</a>, and served as <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consul</a> in 63 BC.
</p><p>His influence on the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> language was immense. He wrote more than three-quarters of extant Latin literature that is known to have existed in his lifetime, and it has been said that subsequent prose was either a reaction against or a return to his style, not only in Latin but in European languages up to the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison2008_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harrison2008-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inc1995_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inc1995-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> Cicero introduced into Latin the arguments of the chief schools of <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_philosophy" title="Hellenistic philosophy">Hellenistic philosophy</a> and created a Latin philosophical vocabulary with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neologisms" title="Neologisms">neologisms</a> such as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">evidentia</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Humanitas" title="Humanitas">humanitas</a></i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">qualitas</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">quantitas</i></span>, and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">essentia</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> distinguishing himself as a translator and philosopher.
</p><p>Though he was an accomplished orator and successful lawyer, Cicero believed his political career was his most important achievement. It was during his consulship that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Catilinarian_conspiracy" title="Second Catilinarian conspiracy">second Catilinarian conspiracy</a> attempted to overthrow the government through an attack on the city by outside forces, and Cicero suppressed the revolt by summarily and controversially executing five conspirators without trial. During the chaotic middle period of the first century BC, marked by <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_civil_wars_and_revolts" title="List of Roman civil wars and revolts">civil wars</a> and the dictatorship of <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>, Cicero championed a return to the traditional republican government. Following Caesar's death, Cicero became an enemy of <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a> in the ensuing power struggle, attacking him in a <a href="/wiki/Philippicae" title="Philippicae">series of speeches</a>. He was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proscribed" title="Proscribed">proscribed</a> as an enemy of the state by the <a href="/wiki/Second_Triumvirate" title="Second Triumvirate">Second Triumvirate</a> and consequently executed by soldiers operating on their behalf in 43 BC having been intercepted during an attempted flight from the Italian peninsula. His severed hands and head were then, as a final revenge of Mark Antony, displayed on the <a href="/wiki/Rostra" title="Rostra">Rostra</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a>'s rediscovery of Cicero's letters is often credited for initiating the 14th-century <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> in <a href="/wiki/Res_publica" title="Res publica">public affairs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_humanism" title="Renaissance humanism">humanism</a>, and classical Roman culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wootton1996_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wootton1996-13">[12]</a></sup> According to Polish historian <a href="/wiki/Tadeusz_Stefan_Zieli%C5%84ski" title="Tadeusz Stefan Zieliński">Tadeusz Zieliński</a>, "the Renaissance was above all things a revival of Cicero, and only after him and through him of the rest of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical antiquity</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> The peak of Cicero's authority and prestige came during the 18th-century <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> and his impact on leading Enlightenment thinkers and political theorists such as <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Burke" title="Edmund Burke">Edmund Burke</a> was substantial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> His works rank among the most influential in global culture, and today still constitute one of the most important bodies of primary material for the writing and revision of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Rome" title="History of Rome">Roman history</a>, especially the last days of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Personal_life_of_Cicero" title="Personal life of Cicero">Personal life of Cicero</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Marcus Tullius Cicero was born on 3 January 106 BC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arpinum" title="Arpinum">Arpinum</a>, a hill town 100 kilometers (62 mi) southeast of Rome. He belonged to the <i><a href="/wiki/Roman_tribe" title="Roman tribe">tribus</a></i> Cornelia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> His father was a well-to-do member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equestrian_(Roman)" title="Equestrian (Roman)">equestrian order</a> and possessed good connections in Rome.  However, being a semi-invalid, he could not enter public life and studied extensively to compensate. Although little is known about Cicero's mother, Helvia, it was common for the wives of important Roman citizens to be responsible for the management of the household. Cicero's brother <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Tullius_Cicero" title="Quintus Tullius Cicero">Quintus</a> wrote in a letter that she was a thrifty housewife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero's <a href="/wiki/Cognomen" title="Cognomen">cognomen</a>, or personal surname, comes from the Latin for <a href="/wiki/Chickpea" title="Chickpea">chickpea</a>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">cicer</i></span>. <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> explains that the name was originally given to one of Cicero's ancestors who had a cleft in the tip of his nose resembling a chickpea. However, it is more likely that Cicero's ancestors prospered through the cultivation and sale of chickpeas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Romans often chose down-to-earth personal surnames. The famous family names of <a href="/wiki/Fabia_gens" title="Fabia gens">Fabius</a>, <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Lentulus_(disambiguation)" title="Lentulus (disambiguation)">Lentulus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Calpurnia_gens#Calpurnii_Pisones" title="Calpurnia gens">Piso</a> come from the Latin names of beans, lentils, and peas, respectively. Plutarch writes that Cicero was urged to change this deprecatory name when he entered politics, but refused, saying that he would make <i>Cicero</i> more glorious than <i><a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aemilius_Scaurus_(consul_115_BC)" title="Marcus Aemilius Scaurus (consul 115 BC)">Scaurus</a></i> ("Swollen-ankled") and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaius_Lutatius_Catulus" title="Gaius Lutatius Catulus">Catulus</a></i> ("Puppy").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Young_Cicero_Reading.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="748" data-file-width="985" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/The_Young_Cicero_Reading.jpg/220px-The_Young_Cicero_Reading.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/The_Young_Cicero_Reading.jpg/330px-The_Young_Cicero_Reading.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/The_Young_Cicero_Reading.jpg/440px-The_Young_Cicero_Reading.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Young_Cicero_Reading.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Young_Cicero_Reading" title="The Young Cicero Reading">The Young Cicero Reading</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Foppa" title="Vincenzo Foppa">Vincenzo Foppa</a> (fresco, 1464), now at the <a href="/wiki/Wallace_Collection" title="Wallace Collection">Wallace Collection</a></div></div></div>
<p>During this period in Roman history, "cultured" meant being able to speak both Latin and Greek. Cicero was therefore educated in the teachings of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek philosophers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_literature" title="Ancient Greek literature">poets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenic_historiography" title="Hellenic historiography">historians</a>; as he obtained much of his understanding of the theory and practice of <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a> from the Greek poet <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Licinius_Archias" title="Aulus Licinius Archias">Archias</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> and from the Greek <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetorician</a> <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_Molon" title="Apollonius Molon">Apollonius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> Cicero used his knowledge of Greek to translate many of the theoretical concepts of Greek philosophy into Latin, thus translating Greek philosophical works for a larger audience. It was precisely his broad education that tied him to the traditional Roman elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-everitt_p35_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-everitt_p35-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero's interest in philosophy figured heavily in his later career and led to him providing a comprehensive account of Greek philosophy for a Roman audience,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> including creating a philosophical vocabulary in Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> In 87 BC, <a href="/wiki/Philo_of_Larissa" title="Philo of Larissa">Philo of Larissa</a>, the head of the <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Platonic Academy</a> that had been founded by <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> in Athens about 300 years earlier, arrived in Rome. Cicero, "inspired by an extraordinary zeal for philosophy",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> sat enthusiastically at his feet and absorbed <a href="/wiki/Carneades" title="Carneades">Carneades</a>' <a href="/wiki/Academic_skepticism" title="Academic skepticism">Academic Skeptic</a> philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>
Cicero said of Plato's Dialogues, that if Zeus were to speak, he would use their language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Plutarch, Cicero was an extremely talented student, whose learning attracted attention from all over Rome,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> affording him the opportunity to study Roman law under <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Mucius_Scaevola_Augur" title="Quintus Mucius Scaevola Augur">Quintus Mucius Scaevola</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> Cicero's fellow students were Gaius Marius Minor, <a href="/wiki/Servius_Sulpicius_Rufus" title="Servius Sulpicius Rufus">Servius Sulpicius Rufus</a> (who became a famous lawyer, one of the few whom Cicero considered superior to himself in legal matters), and <a href="/wiki/Titus_Pomponius_Atticus" title="Titus Pomponius Atticus">Titus Pomponius</a>. The latter two became Cicero's friends for life, and Pomponius (who later received the nickname "Atticus", and whose sister married Cicero's brother) would become, in Cicero's own words, "as a second brother", with both maintaining a lifelong correspondence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-everitt_p35_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-everitt_p35-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 79 BC, Cicero left for Greece, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a>. This was perhaps to avoid the potential wrath of <a href="/wiki/Sulla" title="Sulla">Sulla</a>, as Plutarch claims,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> though Cicero himself says it was to hone his skills and improve his physical fitness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> he studied philosophy with <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_of_Ascalon" title="Antiochus of Ascalon">Antiochus of Ascalon</a>, the 'Old Academic' and initiator of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Platonism" title="Middle Platonism">Middle Platonism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> In Asia Minor, he met the leading orators of the region and continued to study with them. Cicero then journeyed to <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a> to meet his former teacher, <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_Molon" title="Apollonius Molon">Apollonius Molon</a>, who had previously taught him in Rome. Molon helped Cicero hone the excesses in his style, as well as train his body and lungs for the demands of public speaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> Charting a middle path between the competing <a href="/wiki/Atticism" title="Atticism">Attic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asiatic_style" title="Asiatic style">Asiatic styles</a>, Cicero would ultimately become considered second only to <a href="/wiki/Demosthenes" title="Demosthenes">Demosthenes</a> among history's orators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Porta_Saturno,_Quartiere_Colle,Arpino_.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Porta_Saturno%2C_Quartiere_Colle%2CArpino_.jpg/220px-Porta_Saturno%2C_Quartiere_Colle%2CArpino_.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Porta_Saturno%2C_Quartiere_Colle%2CArpino_.jpg/330px-Porta_Saturno%2C_Quartiere_Colle%2CArpino_.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Porta_Saturno%2C_Quartiere_Colle%2CArpino_.jpg/440px-Porta_Saturno%2C_Quartiere_Colle%2CArpino_.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Porta_Saturno,_Quartiere_Colle,Arpino_.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arpino" title="Arpino">Arpino</a>, Italy, birthplace of Cicero</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cicero married <a href="/wiki/Terentia" title="Terentia">Terentia</a> probably at the age of 27, in 79 BC. According to the upper class <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mores#English" title="wikt:mores">mores</a> of the day it was a marriage of convenience, but lasted harmoniously for nearly 30 years. Terentia's family was wealthy, probably the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebs" title="Plebs">plebeian</a> noble house of Terenti Varrones, thus meeting the needs of Cicero's political ambitions in both economic and social terms. She had a half-sister named Fabia, who as a child had become a <a href="/wiki/Vestal_Virgin" title="Vestal Virgin">Vestal Virgin</a>, a great honour. Terentia was a strong willed woman and (citing Plutarch) "she took more interest in her husband's political career than she allowed him to take in household affairs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 50s BC, Cicero's letters to Terentia became shorter and colder. He complained to his friends that Terentia had betrayed him but did not specify in which sense. Perhaps the marriage simply could not outlast the strain of the political upheaval in Rome, Cicero's involvement in it, and various other disputes between the two. The divorce appears to have taken place in 51 BC or shortly before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> In 46 or 45 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Cicero married a young girl, Publilia, who had been his <a href="/wiki/Ward_(law)" title="Ward (law)">ward</a>. It is thought that Cicero needed her money, particularly after having to repay the <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a> of Terentia, who came from a wealthy family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rawson_1975_225_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rawson_1975_225-42">[41]</a></sup> This marriage did not last long.
</p><p>Although his marriage to Terentia was one of convenience, it is commonly known that Cicero held great love for his daughter <a href="/wiki/Tullia_(daughter_of_Cicero)" title="Tullia (daughter of Cicero)">Tullia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> When she suddenly became ill in February 45 BC and died after having seemingly recovered from giving birth to a son in January, Cicero was stunned. "I have lost the one thing that bound me to life" he wrote to Atticus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haskell_1964_249_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haskell_1964_249-44">[43]</a></sup> Atticus told him to come for a visit during the first weeks of his bereavement, so that he could comfort him when his pain was at its greatest. In Atticus's large library, Cicero read everything that the Greek philosophers had written about overcoming grief, "but my sorrow defeats all consolation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> Caesar and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Junius_Brutus_the_Younger" title="Marcus Junius Brutus the Younger">Brutus</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Servius_Sulpicius_Rufus" title="Servius Sulpicius Rufus">Servius Sulpicius Rufus</a> sent him letters of condolence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero hoped that his son <a href="/wiki/Cicero_Minor" title="Cicero Minor">Marcus</a> would become a philosopher like him, but Marcus himself wished for a military career. He joined the army of <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> in 49 BC and after Pompey's defeat at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pharsalus" title="Battle of Pharsalus">Pharsalus</a> 48 BC, he was pardoned by Caesar. Cicero sent him to Athens to study as a disciple of the <a href="/wiki/Peripatetic_school" title="Peripatetic school">peripatetic</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Cratippus_of_Pergamon" title="Cratippus of Pergamon">Kratippos</a> in 48 BC, but he used this absence from "his father's vigilant eye" to "eat, drink and be merry."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> After Cicero's death he joined the army of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberatores" title="Liberatores">Liberatores</a></i> but was later pardoned by <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>. Augustus's bad conscience for not having objected to Cicero's being put on the <a href="/wiki/Proscription" title="Proscription">proscription</a> list during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Triumvirate" title="Second Triumvirate">Second Triumvirate</a> led him to aid considerably Marcus Minor's career. He became an <a href="/wiki/Augur" title="Augur">augur</a>, and was nominated <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consul</a> in 30 BC together with Augustus. As such, he was responsible for revoking the honors of <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a>, who was responsible for the proscription, and could in this way take revenge. Later he was appointed <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">proconsul</a> of <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and the province of Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_career">Public career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Public career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Political_career_of_Cicero" title="Political career of Cicero">Political career of Cicero</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_legal_activity">Early legal activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early legal activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cicero wanted to pursue a public career in politics along the steps of the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Cursus_honorum" title="Cursus honorum">cursus honorum</a></i></span>. In 90–88 BC, he served both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pompeius_Strabo" title="Pompeius Strabo">Pompeius Strabo</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Sulla" title="Lucius Cornelius Sulla">Lucius Cornelius Sulla</a> as they campaigned in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_War_(91%E2%80%9388_BC)" title="Social War (91–88 BC)">Social War</a>, though he had no taste for military life, being an intellectual first and foremost.
</p><p>Cicero started his career as a lawyer around 83–81 BC. The first extant speech is a private case from 81 BC (the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Pro_Quinctio" title="Pro Quinctio">pro Quinctio</a></i></span>), delivered when Cicero was aged 26, though he refers throughout to previous defenses he had already undertaken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> His first major public case, of which a written record is still extant, was his 80 BC defense of <a href="/wiki/Sextus_Roscius" title="Sextus Roscius">Sextus Roscius</a> on the charge of <a href="/wiki/Patricide" title="Patricide">patricide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> Taking this case was a courageous move for Cicero; patricide was considered an appalling crime, and the people whom Cicero accused of the murder, the most notorious being <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Chrysogonus" title="Lucius Cornelius Chrysogonus">Chrysogonus</a>, were favorites of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Sulla_Felix" title="Lucius Cornelius Sulla Felix">Sulla</a>. At this time it would have been easy for Sulla to have the unknown Cicero murdered. Cicero's defense was an indirect challenge to the dictator Sulla, and on the strength of his case, Roscius was acquitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> Soon after, Cicero again challenged Sulla, by criticising his disenfranchisement of Italian towns in a lost speech on behalf of a woman from <a href="/wiki/Arezzo" title="Arezzo">Arretium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero's case in the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Pro_Roscio_Amerino" title="Pro Roscio Amerino">Pro Roscio Amerino</a></i></span> was divided into three parts. The first part detailed exactly the charge brought by Ericius. Cicero explained how a rustic son of a farmer, who lives off the pleasures of his own land, would not have gained anything from committing patricide because he would have eventually inherited his father's land anyway. The second part concerned the boldness and greed of two of the accusers, Magnus and Capito. Cicero told the jury that they were the more likely perpetrators of murder because the two were greedy, both for conspiring together against a fellow kinsman and, in particular, Magnus, for his boldness and for being unashamed to appear in court to support the false charges. The third part explained that Chrysogonus had immense political power, and the accusation was successfully made due to that power. Even though Chrysogonus may not have been what Cicero said he was, through rhetoric Cicero successfully made him appear to be a foreign freed man who prospered by devious means in the aftermath of the civil war. Cicero surmised that it showed what kind of a person he was and that something like murder was not beneath him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career">Early political career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early political career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>His first office was as one of the twenty annual <a href="/wiki/Quaestor" title="Quaestor">quaestors</a>, a training post for serious public administration in a diversity of areas, but with a traditional emphasis on administration and rigorous accounting of public monies under the guidance of a senior magistrate or provincial commander. Cicero served as quaestor in western <a href="/wiki/Sicilia_(Roman_province)" title="Sicilia (Roman province)">Sicily</a> in 75 BC and demonstrated honesty and integrity in his dealings with the inhabitants. As a result, the grateful Sicilians asked Cicero to prosecute <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Verres" title="Gaius Verres">Gaius Verres</a>, a governor of Sicily, who had badly plundered the province. His prosecution of Gaius Verres was a great forensic success<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> for Cicero. Governor Gaius Verres hired the prominent lawyer of a noble family <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Hortensius" title="Quintus Hortensius">Quintus Hortensius Hortalus</a>. After a lengthy period in Sicily collecting testimonials and evidence and persuading witnesses to come forward, Cicero returned to Rome and won the case in a series of dramatic court battles. His unique style of oratory set him apart from the flamboyant Hortensius. On the conclusion of this case, Cicero came to be considered the greatest orator in Rome. The view that Cicero may have taken the case for reasons of his own is viable. Hortensius was, at this point, known as the best lawyer in Rome; to beat him would guarantee much success and the prestige that Cicero needed to start his career. Cicero's oratorical skill is shown in his <a href="/wiki/Character_assassination" title="Character assassination">character assassination</a> of Verres and various other techniques of persuasion used on the jury. One such example is found in the speech <i><a href="/wiki/In_Verrem" title="In Verrem">Against Verres I</a></i>, where he states "with you on this bench, gentlemen, with <a href="/wiki/Manius_Acilius_Glabrio_(consul_67_BC)" title="Manius Acilius Glabrio (consul 67 BC)">Marcus Acilius Glabrio</a> as your president, I do not understand what Verres can hope to achieve".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> Oratory was considered a great art in ancient Rome and an important tool for disseminating knowledge and promoting oneself in elections, in part because there were no regular newspapers or mass media. Cicero was neither a <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">patrician</a> nor a plebeian <a href="/wiki/Nobiles" title="Nobiles">noble</a>; his rise to political office despite his relatively humble origins has traditionally been attributed to his brilliance as an orator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero grew up in a time of civil unrest and war. <a href="/wiki/Sulla" title="Sulla">Sulla</a>'s victory in the first of a series of civil wars led to a new constitutional framework that undermined <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Libertas" title="Libertas">libertas</a></i></span> (liberty), the fundamental value of the Roman Republic. Nonetheless, Sulla's reforms strengthened the position of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equestrian_(Roman)" title="Equestrian (Roman)">equestrian</a> class, contributing to that class's growing political power. Cicero was both an Italian <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">eques</i></span> and a <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Novus_homo" title="Novus homo">novus homo</a></i></span>, but more importantly he was a <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Roman_Republic" title="Constitution of the Roman Republic">Roman constitutionalist</a>. His social class and loyalty to the Republic ensured that he would "command the support and confidence of the people as well as the Italian middle classes". The <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optimates" title="Optimates">optimates</a></i></span> faction never truly accepted Cicero, and this undermined his efforts to reform the Republic while preserving the constitution. Nevertheless, he successfully ascended the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Cursus_honorum" title="Cursus honorum">cursus honorum</a></i></span>, holding each magistracy at or near the youngest possible age: <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Quaestor" title="Quaestor">quaestor</a></i></span> in 75 BC (age 30), <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Aedile" title="Aedile">aedile</a></i></span> in 69 BC (age 36), and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Praetor" title="Praetor">praetor</a></i></span> in 66 BC (age 39), when he served as president of the "Reclamation" (or extortion) Court. He was then elected <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consul</a></i></span> at age 42.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consulship">Consulship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Consulship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina,_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg/300px-Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg/450px-Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg/600px-Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina%2C_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cicer%C3%B3n_denuncia_a_Catilina,_por_Cesare_Maccari.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Cicero Denounces Catiline</i>, <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cesare_Maccari" title="Cesare Maccari">Cesare Maccari</a>, 1882–88</div></div></div>
<p>Cicero, seizing the opportunity offered by optimate fear of reform, was elected consul for the year 63 BC;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JLP106_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-JLP106-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199442_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199442-59">[58]</a></sup> he was elected with the support of every unit of the <a href="/wiki/Centuriate_Assembly" title="Centuriate Assembly">centuriate assembly</a>, rival members of the post-Sullan establishment, and the leaders of municipalities throughout post–Social War Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199442_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199442-59">[58]</a></sup> His co-consul for the year, <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Antonius_Hybrida" title="Gaius Antonius Hybrida">Gaius Antonius Hybrida</a>, played a minor role.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>He began his consular year by opposing a land bill proposed by a plebeian tribune which would have appointed commissioners with semi-permanent authority over land reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199443_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199443-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JLP106_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-JLP106-58">[57]</a></sup> Cicero was also active in the courts, defending <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Rabirius_(senator)" title="Gaius Rabirius (senator)">Gaius Rabirius</a> from accusations of participating in the unlawful killing of plebeian tribune <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Appuleius_Saturninus" title="Lucius Appuleius Saturninus">Lucius Appuleius Saturninus</a> in 100 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199444_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199444-61">[60]</a></sup> The prosecution occurred before the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">comita centuriata</i></span> and threatened to reopen conflict between the Marian and Sullan factions at Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199444_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199444-61">[60]</a></sup> Cicero defended the use of force as being authorised by a <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">senatus consultum ultimum</i></span>, which would prove similar to his own use of force under such conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199444_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199444-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Catilinarian_Conspiracy">The Catilinarian Conspiracy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The Catilinarian Conspiracy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Catilinarian_conspiracy" title="Second Catilinarian conspiracy">Second Catilinarian conspiracy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="188" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Cicero_-_First_speech_against_Catilina_LATIN_(Quo_usque_tandem_abutere,_Catilina..).webm" height="124" id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Cicero_-_First_speech_against_Catilina_LATIN_%28Quo_usque_tandem_abutere%2C_Catilina..%29.webm/220px--Cicero_-_First_speech_against_Catilina_LATIN_%28Quo_usque_tandem_abutere%2C_Catilina..%29.webm.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="321504" data-framerate="29.976" data-height="480" data-shorttitle="VP9 480P" data-title="SD VP9 (480P)" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d5/Cicero_-_First_speech_against_Catilina_LATIN_%28Quo_usque_tandem_abutere%2C_Catilina..%29.webm/Cicero_-_First_speech_against_Catilina_LATIN_%28Quo_usque_tandem_abutere%2C_Catilina..%29.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm;
