<p>Most famously – in part because of his own publicity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199442_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199442-59">[58]</a></sup> – he thwarted a conspiracy led by <a href="/wiki/Catiline" title="Catiline">Lucius Sergius Catilina</a> to overthrow the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> with the help of foreign armed forces. Cicero procured a <i><a href="/wiki/Senatus_consultum_ultimum" title="Senatus consultum ultimum">senatus consultum ultimum</a></i> (a recommendation from the senate attempting to legitimise the use of force)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199442_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199442-59">[58]</a></sup> and drove Catiline from the city with four vehement speeches (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catiline_Orations" title="Catiline Orations">Catiline Orations</a>), which to this day remain outstanding examples of his rhetorical style. The Orations listed Catiline and his followers' debaucheries, and denounced Catiline's senatorial sympathizers as roguish and dissolute debtors clinging to Catiline as a final and desperate hope. Cicero demanded that Catiline and his followers leave the city. At the conclusion of Cicero's first speech (which was made in the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Jupiter_Stator_(3rd_century_BC)" title="Temple of Jupiter Stator (3rd century BC)">Temple of Jupiter Stator</a>), Catiline hurriedly left the Senate. In his following speeches, Cicero did not directly address Catiline. He delivered the second and third orations before the <a href="/wiki/SPQR" title="SPQR">people</a>, and the last one again before the Senate. By these speeches, Cicero wanted to prepare the Senate for the worst possible case; he also delivered more evidence, against Catiline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Catiline fled and left behind his followers to start the revolution from within while he himself assaulted the city with an army of "moral bankrupts and honest fanatics". It is alleged that Catiline had attempted to involve the <a href="/wiki/Allobroges" title="Allobroges">Allobroges</a>, a tribe of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transalpine_Gaul" title="Transalpine Gaul">Transalpine Gaul</a>, in their plot, but Cicero, working with the Gauls, was able to seize letters that incriminated the five conspirators and forced them to confess in front of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">Senate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> The senate then deliberated upon the conspirators' punishment. As it was the dominant advisory body to the various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legislative" title="Legislative">legislative</a> assemblies rather than a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judicial" title="Judicial">judicial</a> body, there were limits to its power; however, martial law was in effect, and it was feared that simple house arrest or exile – the standard options – would not remove the threat to the state. At first <a href="/wiki/Decimus_Junius_Silanus_(consul)" title="Decimus Junius Silanus (consul)">Decimus Junius Silanus</a> spoke for the "extreme penalty"; many were swayed by Julius Caesar, who decried the precedent it would set and argued in favor of life imprisonment in various Italian towns. <a href="/wiki/Cato_the_Younger" title="Cato the Younger">Cato the Younger</a> rose in defense of the death penalty and the entire Senate finally agreed on the matter. Cicero had the conspirators taken to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tullianum" title="Tullianum">Tullianum</a>, the notorious Roman prison, where they were strangled. Cicero himself accompanied the former consul <a href="/wiki/Publius_Cornelius_Lentulus_Sura" title="Publius Cornelius Lentulus Sura">Publius Cornelius Lentulus Sura</a>, one of the conspirators, to the Tullianum.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Cicero received the honorific "<i><a href="/wiki/Pater_Patriae" title="Pater Patriae">pater patriae</a></i>" for his efforts to suppress the conspiracy, but lived thereafter in fear of trial or exile for having put Roman citizens to death without trial.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> While the <i><a href="/wiki/Senatus_consultum_ultimum" title="Senatus consultum ultimum">senatus consultum ultimum</a></i> gave some legitimacy to the use of force against the conspirators,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[b]</a></sup> Cicero also argued that Catiline's conspiracy, by virtue of its treason, made the conspirators enemies of the state and forfeited the protections intrinsically possessed by Roman citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199444_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199444-61">[60]</a></sup> The consuls moved decisively. Antonius Hybrida was dispatched to defeat Catiline in battle that year, preventing Crassus or Pompey from exploiting the situation for their own political aims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199446_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199446-65">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the suppression of the conspiracy, Cicero was proud of his accomplishment.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some of his political enemies argued that though the act gained Cicero popularity, he exaggerated the extent of his success. He overestimated his popularity again several years later after being exiled from Italy and then allowed back from exile. At this time, he claimed that the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">republic</a> would be restored along with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup> Many Romans at the time, led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Populares" title="Populares">Populares</a> politicians <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Gaius Julius Caesar</a> and patrician turned plebeian <a href="/wiki/Publius_Clodius_Pulcher" title="Publius Clodius Pulcher">Publius Clodius Pulcher</a> believed that Cicero's evidence against Catiline was fabricated and the witnesses were bribed. Cicero, who had been elected consul with the support of the Optimates, promoted their position as advocates of the status quo resisting social changes, especially more privileges for the average inhabitants of Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly after completing his consulship, in late 62 BC, Cicero arranged the purchase of a large townhouse on the <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Hill" title="Palatine Hill">Palatine Hill</a> previously owned by Rome's richest citizen, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Licinius_Crassus" title="Marcus Licinius Crassus">Marcus Licinius Crassus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199447_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199447-68">[66]</a></sup> It cost an exorbitant sum, 3.5 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesterces" title="Sesterces">sesterces</a>, which required Cicero to arrange for a loan from his co-consul <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Antonius_Hybrida" title="Gaius Antonius Hybrida">Gaius Antonius Hybrida</a> based on the expected profits from Antonius's proconsulship in Macedonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dustan,_William,_163-164_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dustan,_William,_163-164-70">[68]</a></sup>  At the beginning of his consulship, Cicero had made an arrangement with Hybrida to grant Hybrida the profitable province of Macedonia that had been granted to Cicero by the Senate in exchange for Hybrida staying out of Cicero's way for the year and a quarter of the profits from the province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dustan,_William,_163-164_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dustan,_William,_163-164-70">[68]</a></sup> In return Cicero gained a lavish house which he proudly boasted was <i>"in conspectu prope totius urbis"</i> (in sight of nearly the whole city), only a short walk away from the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Forum" title="Roman Forum">Roman Forum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cicero_house_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-cicero_house-71">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exile_and_return">Exile and return</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Exile and return">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 60 BC, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> invited Cicero to be the fourth member of his existing partnership with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Pompeius_Magnus" title="Gnaeus Pompeius Magnus">Pompey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Licinius_Crassus" title="Marcus Licinius Crassus">Marcus Licinius Crassus</a>, an assembly that would eventually be called the <a href="/wiki/First_Triumvirate" title="First Triumvirate">First Triumvirate</a>. Cicero refused the invitation because he suspected it would undermine the Republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>During Caesar's consulship of 59 BC, the triumvirate had achieved many of their goals of land reform, publicani debt forgiveness, ratification of Pompeian conquests, etc. With Caesar leaving for his provinces, they wished to maintain their stranglehold on politics. They engineered the adoption of patrician <a href="/wiki/Publius_Clodius_Pulcher" title="Publius Clodius Pulcher">Publius Clodius Pulcher</a> into a plebeian family and had him elected as one of the ten <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tribunes_of_the_plebs" title="Tribunes of the plebs">tribunes of the plebs</a> for 58 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199451_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199451-73">[71]</a></sup> Clodius used the triumvirate's backing to push through legislation that benefited them all. He introduced several laws (the <i><a href="/wiki/Leges_Clodiae" title="Leges Clodiae">leges Clodiae</a></i>) that made him very popular with the people, strengthening his power base, then he turned on Cicero by threatening exile to anyone who executed a Roman citizen without a trial. Cicero, having executed members of the Catiline conspiracy four years previously without formal trial, was clearly the intended target.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199450_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199450-74">[72]</a></sup> Furthermore, many believed that Clodius acted in concert with the triumvirate who feared that Cicero would seek to abolish many of Caesar's accomplishments while consul the year before. Cicero argued that the <i><a href="/wiki/Senatus_consultum_ultimum" title="Senatus consultum ultimum">senatus consultum ultimum</a></i> indemnified him from punishment, and he attempted to gain the support of the senators and consuls, especially of Pompey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero grew out his hair, dressed in mourning and toured the streets. Clodius' gangs dogged him, hurling abuse, stones and even excrement. Hortensius, trying to rally to his old rival's support, was almost lynched. The Senate and the consuls were cowed. Caesar, who was still encamped near Rome, was apologetic but said he could do nothing when Cicero brought himself to grovel in the proconsul's tent. Everyone seemed to have abandoned Cicero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>After Clodius passed a law to deny to Cicero fire and water (i.e. shelter) within four hundred miles of Rome, Cicero went into exile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199450_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199450-74">[72]</a></sup> He arrived at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thessalonica" title="Thessalonica">Thessalonica</a>, on 23 May 58 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> In his absence, Clodius, who lived next door to Cicero on the Palatine, arranged for Cicero's house to be confiscated by the state, and was even able to purchase a part of the property in order to extend his own house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cicero_house_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-cicero_house-71">[69]</a></sup> After demolishing Cicero's house, Clodius had the land consecrated and symbolically erected a temple of <a href="/wiki/Libertas" title="Libertas">Liberty</a> (<i>aedes Libertatis</i>) on the vacant spot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero's exile caused him to fall into depression. He wrote to <a href="/wiki/Titus_Pomponius_Atticus" title="Titus Pomponius Atticus">Atticus</a>: "Your pleas have prevented me from committing suicide. But what is there to live for? Don't blame me for complaining. My afflictions surpass any you ever heard of earlier".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> After the intervention of recently elected tribune <a href="/wiki/Titus_Annius_Milo" title="Titus Annius Milo">Titus Annius Milo</a>, acting on the behalf of Pompey who wanted Cicero as a <a href="/wiki/Patronage_in_ancient_Rome" title="Patronage in ancient Rome">client</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199450_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199450-74">[72]</a></sup> the Senate voted in favor of recalling Cicero from exile. Clodius cast the single vote against the decree. Cicero returned to Italy on 5 August 57 BC, landing at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brundisium" title="Brundisium">Brundisium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> He was greeted by a cheering crowd, and, to his delight, his beloved daughter <a href="/wiki/Tullia_(daughter_of_Cicero)" title="Tullia (daughter of Cicero)">Tullia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> In his <i>Oratio De Domo Sua Ad Pontifices</i>, Cicero convinced the <a href="/wiki/College_of_Pontiffs" title="College of Pontiffs">College of Pontiffs</a> to rule that the consecration of his land was invalid, thereby allowing him to regain his property and rebuild his house on the Palatine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero tried to re-enter politics as an independent operator,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199450_74-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199450-74">[72]</a></sup> but his attempts to attack portions of Caesar's legislation were unsuccessful<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199451_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199451-73">[71]</a></sup> and encouraged Caesar to re-solidify his political alliance with Pompey and Crassus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199452_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199452-86">[84]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucca_Conference" title="Lucca Conference">conference at Luca</a> in 56 BC left the three-man alliance in domination of the republic's politics; this forced Cicero to recant and support the triumvirate out of fear from being entirely excluded from public life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199453_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199453-87">[85]</a></sup> After the conference Cicero lavishly praised Caesar's achievements, got the Senate to vote a thanksgiving for Caesar's victories and grant money to pay his troops.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He also delivered a speech 'On the consular provinces' (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">de provinciis consularibus</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199453_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199453-87">[85]</a></sup> which checked an attempt by Caesar's enemies to strip him of his provinces in Gaul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> After this, a cowed Cicero concentrated on his literary works. It is uncertain whether he was directly involved in politics for the following few years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governorship_of_Cilicia">Governorship of Cilicia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Governorship of Cilicia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 51 BC he reluctantly accepted a <a href="/wiki/Promagistrate" title="Promagistrate">promagistracy</a> (as proconsul) in <a href="/wiki/Cilicia_(Roman_province)" title="Cilicia (Roman province)">Cilicia</a> for the year; there were few other former consuls eligible as a result of a legislative requirement enacted by Pompey in 52 BC specifying an interval of five years between a consulship or praetorship and a <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">provincial command</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199459_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199459-90">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> He served as <a href="/wiki/Proconsul" title="Proconsul">proconsul</a> of Cilicia from May 51 BC, arriving in the provinces three months later around August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199459_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199459-90">[88]</a></sup> He was given instructions to keep nearby <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a> loyal to King <a href="/wiki/Ariobarzanes_III_of_Cappadocia" title="Ariobarzanes III of Cappadocia">Ariobarzanes III</a>, which he achieved 'satisfactorily without war'. In 53 BC Marcus Licinius Crassus had been defeated by the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">Battle of Carrhae</a>. This opened up the Roman East for a Parthian invasion, causing much unrest in Syria and Cilicia. Cicero restored calm by his mild system of government. He discovered that a great amount of public property had been embezzled by corrupt previous governors and members of their staff, and did his utmost to restore it. Thus he greatly improved the condition of the cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> He retained the civil rights of, and exempted from penalties, the men who gave the property back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup> Besides this, he was extremely frugal in his outlays for staff and private expenses during his governorship, and this made him highly popular among the natives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> Previous governors had extorted enormous sums from the provincials in order to supply their households and bodyguards.
</p><p>Besides his activity in ameliorating the hard pecuniary situation of the province, Cicero was also creditably active in the military sphere. Early in his governorship he received information that prince <a href="/wiki/Pacorus_I" title="Pacorus I">Pacorus</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Orodes_II" title="Orodes II">Orodes II</a> the king of the Parthians, had crossed the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>, and was ravaging the Syrian countryside and had even besieged <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Cassius_Longinus" title="Gaius Cassius Longinus">Cassius</a> (the interim Roman commander in Syria) in <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup> Cicero eventually marched with two understrength legions and a large contingent of auxiliary cavalry to Cassius's relief. Pacorus and his army had already given up on besieging Antioch and were heading south through Syria, ravaging the countryside again, Cassius and his legions followed them, harrying them wherever they went, eventually ambushing and defeating them near Antigonea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> Another large troop of Parthian horsemen was defeated by Cicero's cavalry who happened to run into them while scouting ahead of the main army. Cicero next defeated some robbers who were based on <a href="/wiki/Nur_Mountains" title="Nur Mountains">Mount Amanus</a> and was hailed as <a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">imperator</a> by his troops. Afterwards he led his army against the independent Cilician mountain tribes, besieging their fortress of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pindenissum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pindenissum (page does not exist)">Pindenissum</a>. It took him 47 days to reduce the place, which fell in December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> On 30 July 50 BC Cicero left the province<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199462_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199462-98">[96]</a></sup> to his brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quintus_Cicero" title="Quintus Cicero">Quintus</a>, who had accompanied him on his governorship as his <a href="/wiki/Legatus" title="Legatus">legate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup> On his way back to Rome he stopped over in <a href="/wiki/Rhodes" title="Rhodes">Rhodes</a> and then went to <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, where he caught up with his old friend <a href="/wiki/Titus_Pomponius_Atticus" title="Titus Pomponius Atticus">Titus Pomponius Atticus</a> and met men of great learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Julius_Caesar.27s_civil_war"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Julius_Caesar's_civil_war">Julius Caesar's civil war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Julius Caesar's civil war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Cicero arrived in Rome on 4 January 49 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199462_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199462-98">[96]</a></sup> He stayed outside the <a href="/wiki/Pomerium" title="Pomerium">pomerium</a>, to retain his promagisterial powers: either in expectation of a triumph or to retain his independent command authority in the coming civil war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199462_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199462-98">[96]</a></sup> The struggle between <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> and Julius Caesar grew more intense in 50 BC. Cicero favored Pompey, seeing him as a defender of the senate and Republican tradition, but at that time avoided openly alienating Caesar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup> When Caesar invaded Italy in 49 BC, Cicero fled Rome. Caesar, seeking an endorsement by a senior senator, courted Cicero's favor, but even so Cicero slipped out of Italy and traveled to Dyrrachium (<a href="/wiki/Epidamnos" title="Epidamnos">Epidamnos</a>), Illyria, where Pompey's staff was situated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup> Cicero traveled with the Pompeian forces to <a href="/wiki/Pharsalus" title="Pharsalus">Pharsalus</a> in 48 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> though he was quickly losing faith in the competence and righteousness of the Pompeian side. Eventually, he provoked the hostility of his fellow senator <a href="/wiki/Cato_the_Younger" title="Cato the Younger">Cato</a>, who told him that he would have been of more use to the cause of the <i>optimates</i> if he had stayed in Rome. After Caesar's victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pharsalus" title="Battle of Pharsalus">Battle of Pharsalus</a> on 9 August, Cicero refused to take command of the Pompeian forces and continue the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199463_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199463-104">[102]</a></sup> He returned to Rome, still as a promagistrate with his lictors, in 47 BC, and dismissed them upon his crossing the pomerium and renouncing his command.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199463_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199463-104">[102]</a></sup> Caesar pardoned him and Cicero tried to adjust to the situation and maintain his political work, hoping that Caesar might revive the Republic and its institutions.
</p><p>In a letter to <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Terentius_Varro" title="Marcus Terentius Varro">Varro</a> on c. 20 April 46 BC, Cicero outlined his strategy under Caesar's dictatorship. Cicero, however, was taken completely by surprise when the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberatores" title="Liberatores">Liberatores</a></i> assassinated Caesar on the <a href="/wiki/Ides_of_March" title="Ides of March">ides of March</a>, 44 BC. Cicero was not included in the conspiracy, even though the conspirators were sure of his sympathy. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Junius_Brutus_the_Younger" title="Marcus Junius Brutus the Younger">Marcus Junius Brutus</a> called out Cicero's name, asking him to restore the republic when he lifted his bloodstained dagger after the assassination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup> A letter Cicero wrote in February 43 BC to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trebonius" title="Trebonius">Trebonius</a>, one of the conspirators, began, "How I could wish that you had invited me to that most glorious banquet on the <a href="/wiki/Ides_of_March" title="Ides of March">Ides of March</a>!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup> Cicero became a popular leader during the period of instability following the assassination. He had no respect for <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Mark Antony</a>, who was scheming to take revenge upon Caesar's murderers. In exchange for amnesty for the assassins, he arranged for the Senate to agree not to declare Caesar to have been a <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrant</a>, which allowed the Caesarians to have lawful support and kept Caesar's reforms and policies intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_to_Mark_Antony_and_death">Opposition to Mark Antony and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Opposition to Mark Antony and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assassinat_de_Cic%C3%A9ron.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="456" data-file-width="529" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Assassinat_de_Cic%C3%A9ron.jpg/220px-Assassinat_de_Cic%C3%A9ron.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Assassinat_de_Cic%C3%A9ron.jpg/330px-Assassinat_de_Cic%C3%A9ron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Assassinat_de_Cic%C3%A9ron.jpg/440px-Assassinat_de_Cic%C3%A9ron.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assassinat_de_Cic%C3%A9ron.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cicero's death (France, 15th century)</div></div></div>
<p>Cicero and Antony now became the two leading men in Rome: Cicero as spokesman for the Senate; Antony as consul, leader of the Caesarian faction, and unofficial executor of Caesar's public will. Relations between the two were never friendly and worsened after Cicero claimed that Antony was taking liberties in interpreting Caesar's wishes and intentions. <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Octavian</a> was Caesar's adopted son and heir. After he returned to Italy, Cicero began to play him against Antony. He praised Octavian, declaring he would not make the same mistakes as his father. He attacked Antony in a series of speeches he called the <a href="/wiki/Philippicae" title="Philippicae"><i>Philippics</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup> after <a href="/wiki/Demosthenes" title="Demosthenes">Demosthenes</a>'s denunciations of <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a>. At the time Cicero's popularity as a public figure was unrivalled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fulvia_y_Marco_Antonio,_o_La_venganza_de_Fulvia_(Museo_del_Prado).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1142" data-file-width="1890" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Fulvia_y_Marco_Antonio%2C_o_La_venganza_de_Fulvia_%28Museo_del_Prado%29.jpg/330px-Fulvia_y_Marco_Antonio%2C_o_La_venganza_de_Fulvia_%28Museo_del_Prado%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Fulvia_y_Marco_Antonio%2C_o_La_venganza_de_Fulvia_%28Museo_del_Prado%29.jpg/495px-Fulvia_y_Marco_Antonio%2C_o_La_venganza_de_Fulvia_%28Museo_del_Prado%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Fulvia_y_Marco_Antonio%2C_o_La_venganza_de_Fulvia_%28Museo_del_Prado%29.jpg/660px-Fulvia_y_Marco_Antonio%2C_o_La_venganza_de_Fulvia_%28Museo_del_Prado%29.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fulvia_y_Marco_Antonio,_o_La_venganza_de_Fulvia_(Museo_del_Prado).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Vengeance of Fulvia</i> by Francisco Maura y Montaner, 1888 depicting Fulvia inspecting the severed head of Cicero</div></div></div>
<p>Cicero supported <a href="/wiki/Decimus_Junius_Brutus_Albinus" title="Decimus Junius Brutus Albinus">Decimus Junius Brutus Albinus</a> as governor of <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaul" title="Cisalpine Gaul">Cisalpine Gaul</a> (<i>Gallia Cisalpina</i>) and urged the Senate to name Antony an enemy of the state. The speech of <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Calpurnius_Piso_Caesoninus_(consul_58_BC)" title="Lucius Calpurnius Piso Caesoninus (consul 58 BC)">Lucius Piso</a>, Caesar's father-in-law, delayed proceedings against Antony. Antony was later declared an <a href="/wiki/Enemy_of_the_state" title="Enemy of the state">enemy of the state</a> when he refused to lift the siege of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutina" title="Mutina">Mutina</a>, which was in the hands of Decimus Brutus. Cicero's plan to drive out Antony failed. Antony and Octavian reconciled and allied with <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aemilius_Lepidus_(triumvir)" title="Marcus Aemilius Lepidus (triumvir)">Lepidus</a> to form the <a href="/wiki/Second_Triumvirate" title="Second Triumvirate">Second Triumvirate</a> after the successive battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Forum_Gallorum" title="Battle of Forum Gallorum">Forum Gallorum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mutina" title="Battle of Mutina">Mutina</a>. The Triumvirate began <a href="/wiki/Proscription" title="Proscription">proscribing</a> their enemies and potential rivals immediately after legislating the alliance into official existence for a term of five years with consular <i><a href="/wiki/Imperium" title="Imperium">imperium</a></i>. Cicero and all of his contacts and supporters were numbered among the enemies of the state, even though Octavian argued for two days against Cicero being added to the list.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero was one of the most viciously and doggedly hunted among the proscribed. He was viewed with sympathy by a large segment of the public and many people refused to report that they had seen him. He was caught on 7 December 43 BC leaving his villa in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formiae" title="Formiae">Formiae</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Litter_(vehicle)" title="Litter (vehicle)">litter</a> heading to the seaside, where he hoped to embark on a ship destined for Macedonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haskell_1964_293_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haskell_1964_293-112">[110]</a></sup> When his killers – Herennius (a Centurion) and Popilius (a Tribune) – arrived, Cicero's own slaves said they had not seen him, but he was given away by Philologus, a <a href="/wiki/Freedman" title="Freedman">freedman</a> of his brother <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Tullius_Cicero" title="Quintus Tullius Cicero">Quintus Cicero</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haskell_1964_293_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Haskell_1964_293-112">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CiceroBust.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="453" data-file-width="314" decoding="async" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/CiceroBust.jpg/220px-CiceroBust.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/23/CiceroBust.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CiceroBust.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cicero about age 60, from a marble bust</div></div></div>
<p>As reported by <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Elder" title="Seneca the Elder">Seneca the Elder</a>, according to the historian <a href="/wiki/Aufidius_Bassus" title="Aufidius Bassus">Aufidius Bassus</a>, Cicero's last words are said to have been:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Ego vero consisto. Accede, veterane, et, si hoc saltim potes recte facere, incide cervicem.</i></span><br/>I go no further: approach, veteran soldier, and, if you can at least do so much properly, sever this neck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>He bowed to his captors, leaning his head out of the litter in a gladiatorial gesture to ease the task. By baring his neck and throat to the soldiers, he was indicating that he would not resist. According to <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, Herennius first slew him, then cut off his head. On Antony's instructions his hands, which had penned the Philippics against Antony, were cut off as well; these were nailed along with his head on the <a href="/wiki/Rostra" title="Rostra">Rostra</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Forum" title="Roman Forum">Forum Romanum</a> according to the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Marius" title="Gaius Marius">Marius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulla" title="Sulla">Sulla</a>, both of whom had displayed the heads of their enemies in the Forum. Cicero was the only victim of the proscriptions who was displayed in that manner. According to <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a>, in a story often mistakenly attributed to Plutarch,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> Antony's wife <a href="/wiki/Fulvia" title="Fulvia">Fulvia</a> took Cicero's head, pulled out his tongue, and jabbed it repeatedly with her hairpin in final revenge against Cicero's power of speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero's son, <a href="/wiki/Cicero_Minor" title="Cicero Minor">Marcus Tullius Cicero Minor</a>, during his year as a consul in 30 BC, avenged his father's death, to a certain extent, when he announced to the Senate Mark Antony's naval defeat at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Actium" title="Battle of Actium">Actium</a> in 31 BC by Octavian and his commander-in-chief, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Vipsanius_Agrippa" title="Marcus Vipsanius Agrippa">Agrippa</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Octavian is reported to have praised Cicero as a patriot and a scholar of meaning in later times, within the circle of his family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup> However, it was Octavian's acquiescence that had allowed Cicero to be killed, as Cicero was condemned by the new triumvirate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero's career as a statesman was marked by inconsistencies and a tendency to shift his position in response to changes in the political climate. His indecision may be attributed to his sensitive and impressionable personality; he was prone to overreaction in the face of political and private change.
"Would that he had been able to endure prosperity with greater self-control, and adversity with more fortitude!" wrote <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaius_Asinius_Pollio_(consul_40_BC)" title="Gaius Asinius Pollio (consul 40 BC)">C. Asinius Pollio</a>, a contemporary Roman statesman and historian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup>
</p>

<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg"><img alt="icon" class="noviewer thumbborder" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Republicanism_sidebar" title="Template:Republicanism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Republicanism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Republicanism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Republicanism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thys_Boke_Is_Myne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="991" decoding="async" height="341" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Thys_Boke_Is_Myne.jpg/220px-Thys_Boke_Is_Myne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Thys_Boke_Is_Myne.jpg/330px-Thys_Boke_Is_Myne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Thys_Boke_Is_Myne.jpg/440px-Thys_Boke_Is_Myne.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thys_Boke_Is_Myne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII's</a> childhood copy of <i><a href="/wiki/De_Officiis" title="De Officiis">De Officiis</a></i>, bearing the inscription in his hand, "Thys boke is myne Prynce Henry"</div></div></div>
<p>Cicero has been traditionally considered the master of Latin prose, with <a href="/wiki/Quintilian" title="Quintilian">Quintilian</a> declaring that Cicero was "not the name of a man, but of eloquence itself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> The English words <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Ciceronian" title="wikt:Ciceronian">Ciceronian</a></i> (meaning "eloquent") and <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cicerone" title="wikt:cicerone">cicerone</a></i> (meaning "local guide") derive from his name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup> He is credited with transforming Latin from a modest utilitarian language into a versatile literary medium capable of expressing abstract and complicated thoughts with clarity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> praised Cicero's achievement by saying "it is more important to have greatly extended the frontiers of the Roman spirit than the frontiers of the Roman empire".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/John_William_Mackail" title="John William Mackail">John William Mackail</a>, "Cicero's unique and imperishable glory is that he created the language of the civilized world, and used that language to create a style which nineteen centuries have not replaced, and in some respects have hardly altered."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero was also an energetic writer with an interest in a wide variety of subjects, in keeping with the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_philosophy" title="Hellenistic philosophy">Hellenistic philosophical</a> and rhetorical traditions in which he was trained. The quality and ready accessibility of Ciceronian texts favored very wide distribution and inclusion in teaching curricula, as suggested by a graffito at Pompeii, admonishing: "You will like Cicero, or you will be whipped".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup>
Cicero was greatly admired by influential <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>, who credited Cicero's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lost_work" title="Lost work">lost</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Hortensius_(Cicero)" title="Hortensius (Cicero)">Hortensius</a></i> for his eventual conversion to Christianity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup> and St. <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a>, who had a feverish vision in which he was accused of being "follower of Cicero and not of Christ" before the judgment seat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup>
This influence further increased after the <a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Early Middle Ages</a> in Europe, which more of his writings survived than any other Latin author. Medieval philosophers were influenced by Cicero's writings on <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a> and innate rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs additional references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">additional citation(s) needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a>'s rediscovery of Cicero's letters provided the impetus for searches for ancient Greek and Latin writings scattered throughout European monasteries, and the subsequent rediscovery of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a> led to the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>. Subsequently, Cicero became synonymous with classical Latin to such an extent that a number of humanist scholars began to assert that no Latin word or phrase should be used unless it appeared in Cicero's works, a stance criticised by <a href="/wiki/Erasmus" title="Erasmus">Erasmus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>His voluminous correspondence, much of it addressed to his friend <a href="/wiki/Titus_Pomponius_Atticus" title="Titus Pomponius Atticus">Atticus</a>, has been especially influential, introducing the art of refined letter writing to European culture. <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Nepos" title="Cornelius Nepos">Cornelius Nepos</a>, the first century BC biographer of Atticus, remarked that Cicero's letters contained such a wealth of detail "concerning the inclinations of leading men, the faults of the generals, and the revolutions in the government" that their reader had little need for a history of the period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among Cicero's admirers were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desiderius_Erasmus" title="Desiderius Erasmus">Desiderius Erasmus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> Following the invention of Johannes Gutenberg's printing press, <i><a href="/wiki/De_Officiis" title="De Officiis">De Officiis</a></i> was the second book printed in Europe, after the <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg_Bible" title="Gutenberg Bible">Gutenberg Bible</a>. Scholars note Cicero's influence on the rebirth of religious toleration in the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero was especially popular with the <a href="/wiki/Philosophes" title="Philosophes">Philosophes</a> of the 18th century, including <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diderot" title="Diderot">Diderot</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> Gibbon wrote of his first experience reading the author's collective works thus: "I tasted the beauty of the language; I breathed the spirit of freedom; and I imbibed from his precepts and examples the public and private sense of a man...after finishing the great author, a library of eloquence and reason, I formed a more extensive plan of reviewing the Latin classics..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> Voltaire called Cicero "the greatest as well as the most elegant of Roman philosophers" and even staged a play based on Cicero's role in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Catilinarian_conspiracy" title="Second Catilinarian conspiracy">Catilinarian conspiracy</a>, called <i>Rome Sauvée, ou Catilina</i>, to "make young people who go to the theatre acquainted with Cicero."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> Voltaire was spurred to pen the drama as a rebuff to his rival <a href="/wiki/Claude_Prosper_Jolyot_de_Cr%C3%A9billon" title="Claude Prosper Jolyot de Crébillon">Claude Prosper Jolyot de Crébillon's</a> own play <i>Catilina</i>, which had portrayed Cicero as a coward and villain who hypocritically married his own daughter to Catiline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthew_Sharpe_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matthew_Sharpe-137">[135]</a></sup> Montesquieu produced his "Discourse on Cicero" in 1717, in which he heaped praise on the author because he rescued "philosophy from the hands of scholars, and freed it from the confusion of a foreign language".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> Montesquieu went on to declare that Cicero was "of all the ancients, the one who had the most personal merit, and whom I would prefer to resemble."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matthew_Sharpe_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Matthew_Sharpe-137">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Internationally, Cicero the republican inspired the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers of the United States</a> and the revolutionaries of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> said, "As all the ages of the world have not produced a greater statesman and philosopher united than Cicero, his authority should have great weight."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup> Jefferson names Cicero as one of a handful of major figures who contributed to a tradition "of public right" that informed his draft of the Declaration of Independence and shaped American understandings of "the common sense" basis for the right of revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Camille_Desmoulins" title="Camille Desmoulins">Camille Desmoulins</a> said of the French republicans in 1789 that they were "mostly young people who, nourished by the reading of Cicero at school, had become passionate enthusiasts for liberty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jim_Powell_(historian)" title="Jim Powell (historian)">Jim Powell</a> starts his book on the history of liberty with the sentence: "Marcus Tullius Cicero expressed principles that became the bedrock of liberty in the modern world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Likewise, no other ancient personality has inspired as much venomous dislike as Cicero, especially in more modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup> His commitment to the values of the Republic accommodated a hatred of the poor and persistent opposition to the advocates and mechanisms of popular representation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a> referred to him as "the most contemptible scoundrel in history" for upholding republican "democracy" while at the same time denouncing land and class reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup> Cicero has faced criticism for exaggerating the democratic qualities of republican Rome, and for defending the Roman oligarchy against the popular reforms of Caesar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Parenti" title="Michael Parenti">Michael Parenti</a> admits Cicero's abilities as an orator, but finds him a vain, pompous and hypocritical personality who, when it suited him, could show public support for popular causes that he privately despised. Parenti presents Cicero's prosecution of the Catiline conspiracy as legally flawed at least, and possibly unlawful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero also had an influence on modern astronomy. <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a>, searching for ancient views on earth motion, said that he "first ... found in Cicero that <a href="/wiki/Hicetas" title="Hicetas">Hicetas</a> supposed the earth to move."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Notably, "Cicero" was the name attributed to size 12 font in typesetting table drawers. For ease of reference, type sizes 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, 10, 12, 14, 16, and 20 were all given different names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marci_Tullii_Ciceronis_Opera_Omnia.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7544" data-file-width="5032" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Marci_Tullii_Ciceronis_Opera_Omnia.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Marci_Tullii_Ciceronis_Opera_Omnia.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Marci_Tullii_Ciceronis_Opera_Omnia.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Marci_Tullii_Ciceronis_Opera_Omnia.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Marci_Tullii_Ciceronis_Opera_Omnia.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Marci_Tullii_Ciceronis_Opera_Omnia.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marci_Tullii_Ciceronis_Opera_Omnia.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Marci Tullii Ciceronis Opera Omnia</i> (1566)</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Writings_of_Cicero" title="Writings of Cicero">Writings of Cicero</a></div>
<p>Cicero was declared a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Righteous_pagan" title="Righteous pagan">righteous pagan</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Chalcedonian_Christianity" title="Chalcedonian Christianity">Early Church</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup> and therefore many of his works were deemed worthy of preservation. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bogomils" title="Bogomils">Bogomils</a> considered him a rare exception of a pagan saint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup> Subsequent Roman and medieval Christian writers quoted liberally from his works <i><a href="/wiki/De_re_publica" title="De re publica">De re publica</a></i> (<i>On the Commonwealth</i>) and <i><a href="/wiki/De_Legibus" title="De Legibus">De Legibus</a></i> (<i>On the Laws</i>), and much of his work has been recreated from these surviving fragments. Cicero also articulated an early, abstract conceptualization of rights, based on ancient law and custom. Of Cicero's books, six on rhetoric have survived, as well as parts of seven on philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herrick_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herrick-154">[152]</a></sup> Of his speeches, 88 were recorded, but only 52 survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[c]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_archaeology">In archaeology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: In archaeology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Cicero's great repute in Italy has led to numerous ruins being identified as having belonged to him, though none have been substantiated with absolute certainty. In <a href="/wiki/Formia" title="Formia">Formia</a>, two Roman-era ruins are popularly believed to be Cicero's mausoleum, the <i>Tomba di Cicerone</i>, and the villa where he was assassinated in 43 BC. The latter building is centered around a central hall with Doric columns and a coffered vault, with a separate <a href="/wiki/Nymphaeum" title="Nymphaeum">nymphaeum</a>, on five acres of land near Formia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup> A modern villa was built on the site after the Rubino family purchased the land from <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_the_Two_Sicilies" title="Ferdinand II of the Two Sicilies">Ferdinand II of the Two Sicilies</a> in 1868. Cicero's supposed tomb is a 24-meter (79 feet) tall tower on an <i><a href="/wiki/Opus_quadratum" title="Opus quadratum">opus quadratum</a></i> base on the ancient Via Appia outside of Formia. Some suggest that it is not in fact Cicero's tomb, but a monument built on the spot where Cicero was intercepted and assassinated while trying to reach the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, a large villa excavated in the mid 18th century just outside the Herculaneum Gate was widely believed to have been Cicero's, who was known to have owned a holiday villa in Pompeii he called his <i>Pompeianum</i>. The villa was stripped of its fine frescoes and mosaics and then re-buried after 1763 – it has yet to be re-excavated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[156]</a></sup> However, contemporaneous descriptions of the building from the excavators combined with Cicero's own references to his <i>Pompeianum</i> differ, making it unlikely that it is Cicero's villa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Rome, the location of Cicero's house has been roughly identified from excavations of the Republican-era stratum on the northwestern slope of the <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Hill" title="Palatine Hill">Palatine Hill</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rome_alive_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-rome_alive-161">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[159]</a></sup> Cicero's <i>domus</i> has long been known to have stood in the area, according to his own descriptions and those of later authors, but there is some debate about whether it stood near the base of the hill, very close to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Forum" title="Roman Forum">Roman Forum</a>, or nearer to the summit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rome_alive_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-rome_alive-161">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[160]</a></sup> During his life the area was the most desirable in Rome, densely occupied with Patrician houses including the <i>Domus Publica</i> of Julius Caesar and the home of Cicero's mortal enemy Clodius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_fictional_portrayals">Notable fictional portrayals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Notable fictional portrayals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante</a>'s 1320 poem the <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a></i>, the author encounters Cicero, among other philosophers, in <a href="/wiki/Limbo" title="Limbo">Limbo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[162]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">Ben Jonson</a> dramatised the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Catilinarian_Conspiracy" title="Second Catilinarian Conspiracy">conspiracy of Catiline</a> in his play <i><a href="/wiki/Catiline_His_Conspiracy" title="Catiline His Conspiracy">Catiline His Conspiracy</a></i>, featuring Cicero as a character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[163]</a></sup> Cicero also appears as a minor character in <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>'s play <i><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(play)" title="Julius Caesar (play)">Julius Caesar</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cicero was portrayed on the motion picture screen by British actor <a href="/wiki/Alan_Napier" title="Alan Napier">Alan Napier</a> in the 1953 film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(1953_film)" title="Julius Caesar (1953 film)">Julius Caesar</a></i>, based on Shakespeare's play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[165]</a></sup> He has also been played by such noted actors as <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hordern" title="Michael Hordern">Michael Hordern</a> (in <i><a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_(1963_film)" title="Cleopatra (1963 film)">Cleopatra</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[166]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Morell" title="André Morell">André Morell</a> (in the 1970 <i><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(1970_film)" title="Julius Caesar (1970 film)">Julius Caesar</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[167]</a></sup> Most recently, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Rome_characters" title="List of Rome characters">Cicero</a> was portrayed by <a href="/wiki/David_Bamber" title="David Bamber">David Bamber</a> in the HBO series <i><a href="/wiki/Rome_(TV_series)" title="Rome (TV series)">Rome</a></i> (2005–2007) and appeared in both seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the historical novel series <i><a href="/wiki/Masters_of_Rome" title="Masters of Rome">Masters of Rome</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Colleen_McCullough" title="Colleen McCullough">Colleen McCullough</a> presents a not-so-flattering depiction of Cicero's career, showing him struggling with an inferiority complex and vanity, morally flexible and fatally indiscreet, while his rival <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> is shown in a more approving light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[169]</a></sup> Cicero is portrayed as a hero in the novel <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=A_Pillar_of_Iron&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="A Pillar of Iron (page does not exist)">A Pillar of Iron</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Caldwell" title="Taylor Caldwell">Taylor Caldwell</a> (1965). <a href="/wiki/Robert_Harris_(novelist)" title="Robert Harris (novelist)">Robert Harris</a>' novels <i><a href="/wiki/Imperium_(Harris_novel)" title="Imperium (Harris novel)">Imperium</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lustrum_(novel)" title="Lustrum (novel)">Lustrum</a></i> (published under the name <i>Conspirata</i> in the United States) and <i><a href="/wiki/Dictator_(Harris_novel)" title="Dictator (Harris novel)">Dictator</a></i> comprise a three-part series based on the life of Cicero. In these novels Cicero's character is depicted in a more favorable way than in those of McCullough, with his positive traits equaling or outweighing his weaknesses (while conversely Caesar is depicted as more sinister than in McCullough).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[170]</a></sup> Cicero is a major recurring character in the <i><a href="/wiki/Roma_Sub_Rosa" title="Roma Sub Rosa">Roma Sub Rosa</a></i> series of mystery novels by <a href="/wiki/Steven_Saylor" title="Steven Saylor">Steven Saylor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[171]</a></sup> He also appears several times as a peripheral character in <a href="/wiki/John_Maddox_Roberts" title="John Maddox Roberts">John Maddox Roberts</a>' <a href="/wiki/SPQR_series" title="SPQR series"><i>SPQR</i> series</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Samuel_Barnett_(actor)" title="Samuel Barnett (actor)">Samuel Barnett</a> portrays Cicero in a 2017 <a href="/wiki/Radio_drama" title="Radio drama">audio drama</a> series pilot produced by <a href="/wiki/Big_Finish_Productions" title="Big Finish Productions">Big Finish Productions</a>.  A full series was released the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[173]</a></sup> All Episodes are written by <a href="/wiki/David_Llewellyn_(author)" title="David Llewellyn (author)">David Llewellyn</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[174]</a></sup> and directed and produced by <a href="/wiki/Scott_Handcock" title="Scott Handcock">Scott Handcock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[175]</a></sup> Llewellyn, Handcock and Barnett re-teamed in the <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">Doctor Who</a> audio-drama <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctor_Who_%E2%80%93_Main_Range#2019" title="Doctor Who – Main Range">Tartarus</a> (also produced by Big Finish) starring <a href="/wiki/Peter_Davison" title="Peter Davison">Peter Davison</a> as <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Doctor" title="Fifth Doctor">the 5th Doctor</a>. It is not intended to be a part of the Cicero series; in Vortex (Big Finish's official free online magazine) Llewellyn revealed that he was "worried that if we had Cicero meeting aliens people might go back to the Cicero series and see it through a <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">sci-fi</a> lens. Then I remembered that <a href="/wiki/Simon_Callow" title="Simon Callow">Simon Callow</a> still performs as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>, and that he played Dickens before reprising him in the Doctor Who TV episode, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Unquiet_Dead" title="The Unquiet Dead">The Unquiet Dead</a></i> – so I got over myself!".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cicero&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-lower-alpha" style="column-width: 50em;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The name is infrequently <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglicized" title="Anglicized">anglicized</a> as <b>Tully</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Wiedemann describes the senatus consultum ultimum by the late republic as "little more than a fig-leaf by those who could muster a majority in the senate ... to legitimate the use of force".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199444_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiedemann199444-61">[60]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-155">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sources vary, but seem to indicate that 52 survived in whole and 6 more in part</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-Harrison2008-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Harrison2008_8-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHarrison2008">Harrison, Stephen (2008). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=RI0ivXkSqjAC" rel="nofollow"><i>A Companion to Latin Literature</i></a>. John Wiley &amp; Sons. p. 31. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1405137379" title="Special:BookSources/978-1405137379"><bdi>978-1405137379</bdi></a>. <q>Latin literature in the period 90–40 BC presents one feature that is unique in Classical, and perhaps even in the whole of Western, literature. Although it is a period from which a substantial amount of literature in a wide variety of genres survives, more than 75 per cent of that literature was written by a single man: Marcus Tullius Cicero. Cicero wrote speeches, philosophical and rhetorical trea- tises, letters and poetry, which simply in terms of quantity outweigh all other extant writings of the period.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+Companion+to+Latin+Literature&amp;rft.pages=31&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.isbn=978-1405137379&amp;rft.aulast=Harrison&amp;rft.aufirst=Stephen&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DRI0ivXkSqjAC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACicero"></span></span>
